<div id="review_1499586235" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1499586235" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1499586235?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906548674" href="/book/show/12265773-the-world-of-yesterday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=memoirs">memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1499586235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15363715809984234867"><b>
  <i>We failed to see the writing on the wall in letters of fire. Like King Belshazzar before us, we dined on the delicious dishes of the arts and never looked apprehensively ahead.</i>
</b><br/><br/>At one point during the first half of the 20th century, two Austrians would take residence in the high and remote corners of the Alps, almost exactly opposite one another. Both were at one time living in the Austrian capital of Vienna, though their experiences have been remarkably different - one wanted to be an artist</span>
  <span id="freeText15363715809984234867" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>We failed to see the writing on the wall in letters of fire. Like King Belshazzar before us, we dined on the delicious dishes of the arts and never looked apprehensively ahead.</i>
</b><br /><br />At one point during the first half of the 20th century, two Austrians would take residence in the high and remote corners of the Alps, almost exactly opposite one another. Both were at one time living in the Austrian capital of Vienna, though their experiences have been remarkably different - one wanted to be an artist, but failed; the other wanted to be an artist, and succeeded. Despite that difference, both of them would venture out to Vienna and become remarkably famous. One of them would lead a movement which would change the world; the other would be applauded and loved for his work across the continents, but later forced into self-exile and banned in his native country. To our detriment, we live in a world shaped by the actions and ideas of the first Austrian, while the other has been quietly pushed away into obscurity and largely forgotten.<br /><br />The world of today continues to exist in the shadow of Adolf Hitler, and all that he came to embody: political fanaticism, blind nationalism and equally blind faith in personal superiority shared by everyone. Although he was one of the most famous writers of the world during his lifetime, Stefan Zweig is little known today . His many romantic stories, novellas and plays have been gathering dust in libraries and disappeared from the world of international publishing; only recently there's been a small resurgence in his work, thanks largely for it being newly translated into English.<br /><br /><i>The World of Yesterday</i> is Zweig's last book, in which he describes the world he had to leave with great love and affection, but also great sadness resulting from certainty that he was seeing its last days. This is not a proper autobiography, since Zweig keeps many details from his personal life away from the reader; it is a combination of a memoir with a travelogue, in which he reminisces about the many places he visited and lived in, and the people he met and was friends with.<br /><br />Vienna, the traditional capital of the Habsburg monarchy and the Austro-Hungarian empire, was one of the most multicultural and populous cities in Europe. A major cultural hub on the continent, the city was home to Franz Schubert, Gustav Mahler, Ludwig Wittgenstein and Sigmund Freud among many others; one could hardly find a more cosmopolitan metropolis in the entire world. Vienna personifies the great divide between Stefan Zweig and Adolf Hitler. Zweig embraced the cosmopolitan city and its culture: he felt at home in its many cafes and artistic venues, embracing the atmosphere of intellectual tolerance, which attracted the most diverse people. A pacifist and firm believer in European unity, freedom of movement and friendship between nations, Zweig considered himself and his fellow Viennese real, supranational citizens of the world. <br /><br />Young Hitler left Linz for Vienna to enroll in the Academy of Fine Arts, where he wanted to learn to paint. Twice rejected, he quickly spent his inheritance and lived in a series of shelters and men's homes, sometimes sleeping rough and having to beg for money. Hitler did not admire the city like Zweig did, considering it to be one of the most backward cities in Europe, where" dazzling riches and loathsome poverty alternated sharply". As he painted postcards of Vienna for pennies to get by, Hitler came to know the face of the city which the bourgeoisie Zweig never could - that of abject and miserable poverty. Hitler later wrote that he associated Vienna only with hardship and misery; it was during this period that he adopted ruthless social darwinism, which would remain the main worldview to the end of his life. Contrary to apolitical Zweig, Hitler developed an intense interest in politics - reading any newspaper or book that he could get his hands on, and engaging in debates with anyone willing to listen to him. Like many Viennese at the time, Hitler also attended debates held by the Austrian parliament, but found them to be a multilingual chaos, a lamentable comedy, a farce. It is also at this time when he began to pick up the odious antisemitism which was common among Viennese pan-Germans, loating the diversity of the city so adored by Zweig; Hitler wrote that it was in Vienna where he discovered "the two menaces" - Marxism and Jewry, and where he ceased to be a weak-kneed cosmopolitan and became an anti-semite.<br /><br />Whereas Hitler sought the establishment of people's community, which would unify all ethnic Germans through National Socialism and ultimately create a racially uniform German Reich, Zweig remained a transnational humanist and a believer in the transcendental power of culture. <i>The World of Yesterday</i> is full of Zweig's excitement at the sheer amount of artistry and creativity present on the continent before the outbreak of the war. Zweig writes with genuine feeling not only about Vienna, but also about Paris, a city which he genuinely loved and despaired when it fell to the Nazis; he also travels to the young Soviet Russia, and to his credit remains skeptical about this great social and political experiment - unlike many other intellectual and writers. He rubs elbows with Theodor Herzl - the founder of modern Zionism, the poet Rainer Maria Rilke, meets James Joyce, attends a poetry reading by W. B. Yeats, witnesses a debate between H.G. Wells and G.B. Shaw, and even claims that Benito Mussolini has personally intervened and pardoned a prisoner after his request. In a cruel twist of historical irony, the moment where European culture is at its highest is also the one where the political situation on the continent is most unstable; Zweig's devotion to European unity, free movement and open exchange of culture and ideas is constantly contrasted by the looming specters of war - first the Great War, and later World War 2, which would drive him out of the continent altogether. <br /><br />Zweig observes the hyperinflation and the rise of nationalistic radicalism, which first removed his native Austria from the map and then reduced the rest of the continent to a burning mass of rubble and death. The later sections of the memoir are the most poignant; Zweig, a real citizen of the world if there ever was one, sees increasingly growing ethnic tensions and political hatred, and is rendered stateless and without a home, forced always to be at the hospitality of others. The man who saw no sense in borders between nations and advocated for international fraternity and friendship was forced to fill mountains of paperwork as he moved between countries, having to apply for endless visas and being treated as an enemy alien; in a deeply poignant moment he mourns the shattering of the prewar dream of a truly free and borderless world, and the human dignity and creativity lost in the spirit-crushing border procedures.<br /><br />Zweig eventually arrived in Brazil, a country which he picked because he saw it as a place still undiscovered, with its immense natural beauty allowing for a possibility of development of an entirely new, untainted civilization. But the shadow of war and destruction of Europe has followed him there,and refused to let go. Tormented by the loss of his country and the world he believed in, Zweig posted the manuscripts of <i>The World of Yesterday</i> and his last novella, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1131361846?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Chess Story</a> to his publisher in New York on February 22, 1942, and committed suicide together with his wife, Lotte, on February 23th. They were found dead in their bed,together, in a final embrace. Three years later, in the last remarkable similarity which their lives would share, Adolf Hitler and Eva Braun would also commit suicide. This would put not only an end to the war in Europe but also a final end to Zweig's World of Yesterday, which would never again exist and where his golden age of security would never resurface, and be instead replaced by the bitter experience and barbed wire of the Cold War, international terrorism instead of international fraternity, with the new looming threat of the nuclear bomb.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15363715809984234867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText15005164733392955712" style="display:none">I was somewhat disappointed in this one, and ended up skipping around a lot. This old (and anonymous) translation is stiff and quite unappealing, and while there are certainly many interesting stories, there is also a looseness in the prose and the book runs on a bit verbosely. <br /><br />There are some very interesting insights in the Hitler chapter, but Zweig soon escaped and lived in relative peace, and so was not the best witness (as he admits) for the events he subsequently lived through. <br /><br />He seems to have been a very sweet and well balanced man, which only makes his sucide seem all the more inexplicable.<br /><br />Anyone interested in this book should definitely spring for the new (Anthea Bell) translation.</span>

<div id="review_2092540142" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2092540142" />
    <a title="Tanja Berg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg"><img alt="Tanja Berg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558953813p2/5935491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2092540142?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanja Berg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanja Berg" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg">Tanja Berg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">EVERYONE</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=biography-memoir">biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2092540142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8400940853832047929">I was so sorry having finished this book that my first instinct was to listen to it again, immediately. I have rarely read such a profoundly insightful book. <br/><br/>Stefan Zweig is an Austrian author, Jewish. Born in 1881, at the height of his career he was one of the most popular writers in the world. I read and enjoyed some of his works in my teens. This, however, is a book I came across on my desperate search for audio books. I took just one brief look, and knew I had to have it.<br/><br/>Stefan wrote this bo</span>
  <span id="freeText8400940853832047929" style="display:none">I was so sorry having finished this book that my first instinct was to listen to it again, immediately. I have rarely read such a profoundly insightful book. <br /><br />Stefan Zweig is an Austrian author, Jewish. Born in 1881, at the height of his career he was one of the most popular writers in the world. I read and enjoyed some of his works in my teens. This, however, is a book I came across on my desperate search for audio books. I took just one brief look, and knew I had to have it.<br /><br />Stefan wrote this book in exile. He had lost everything he owned and his books were banned in Germany, Austria and all other countries where Hitler had power. Although they were available in the free world, Stefan deeply regretted that they could no longer be read in original German. This book, "the world of yesterday", is an autobiography and commentary on the world Stefan lived through. From the end of the 1800's, with their restrictive female fashion, to the roaring 1920's with its hyper-inflation and making of unworthy billionaires, to the rise of Hitler.<br /><br />The book ends when the second world war begins, September 1, 1939. The book was written during the war. This means that some of the atrocities committed by the Nazis weren't even known. What was known was bad enough. Descriptions of how the hate-mongering Hitler got power and the passivity of most people gave me chills. There are just too many similarities to the 1930's to leave me comfortable.<br /><br />This book is a fantastic social commentary by someone who lived through it all. Someone who went from fame to infamy. Who had it all and lost everything, even his nationality. <br /><br />To quote George Santayana: "Those who cannot learn from history are doomed to repeat it." <br /><br />There were some truly insightful quotes by Stefan, but since I listened to the book and haven't highlighted or written them down, I can't share them with you. I will update this review later, when I have bought this on paper and read it again. <br /><br />Read this book. You will learn a lot, easily. The story is vividly and heart-wrenchingly told. There are lessons in this book that we need to learn and remember, if we are to avoid pitfalls in the present day and age.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8400940853832047929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7178235783438487344">As an Autrian-Jewish writer who experienced both World Wars and encountered numerous influencial and interesting people in his life, I expected Zweig would have a facinating story to tell. I found his autobiographical work very boring however, and mostly filled with tediously written descriptions of Zweig's own importance and greatness. The following, almost satirical quote, illustrates my point I think:* <br/><br/><blockquote>Es ist ein unablässiges Ballast-über-Bord werfen, ein ständiges Verdichten und Klären der i</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText7178235783438487344" style="display:none">As an Autrian-Jewish writer who experienced both World Wars and encountered numerous influencial and interesting people in his life, I expected Zweig would have a facinating story to tell. I found his autobiographical work very boring however, and mostly filled with tediously written descriptions of Zweig's own importance and greatness. The following, almost satirical quote, illustrates my point I think:* <br /><br /><blockquote>Es ist ein unablässiges Ballast-über-Bord werfen, ein ständiges Verdichten und Klären der inneren Architektur; während die meisten andern sich nicht entschliessen können, etwas zu verschweigen, was sie wissen, und mit einer gewissen Verliebtheit in jede gelungene Zeile sich weiter und tiefer zeigen wollen, als sie eigentlich sind, ist es mein Ehrgeiz, immer mehr zu wissen, als nach aussen hin sichtbar wird.</blockquote><br /><br /><i>*I read the German version but would be thankful to anyone who can supply the Eng translation</i></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer950370854" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12204739950444228046">This memoir is an exceptionally beautiful piece of art and literature! Stefan Zweig takes his readers on a journey of a lifetime (literally too). He succeeds to capture the Zeitgeist of more than half a century (and what a time indeed: the final years of the 19th century, the Great War and the years leading up to the Second World War) and makes it come alive. It was hard to put the book down before going to sleep at nights. I regretted Having to leave the book untouched for days because of work </span>
  <span id="freeText12204739950444228046" style="display:none">This memoir is an exceptionally beautiful piece of art and literature! Stefan Zweig takes his readers on a journey of a lifetime (literally too). He succeeds to capture the Zeitgeist of more than half a century (and what a time indeed: the final years of the 19th century, the Great War and the years leading up to the Second World War) and makes it come alive. It was hard to put the book down before going to sleep at nights. I regretted Having to leave the book untouched for days because of work yet as soon as I had time again I was immediately drawn back into it by Zweig's wonderful prose. Now I'm done. I finished his memoir but I am not done reading Zweig's work.<br /><br /></span>

  <span id="freeText11151945005177793219" style="display:none">There’s something a bit over-wrought in Zweig’s writing I find. <div id="review_549190784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/549190784" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/549190784?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13015684-the-world-of-yesterday">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone interested in memoirs, European history</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Sue by: </span>
      Kris
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=austria">austria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=bio-memoir">bio-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=wwi">wwi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer549190784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9940929562278515565">I have delayed writing this review in hopes that some inspiration will come (perhaps writing it for me) but no, I do have to perform the task from my own brain. This is perhaps one of the most melancholy memoirs I've read. While Zweig provides an often golden-hued picture of the Europe of his youth, the turn of the century Vienna of the final years of the Hapsburg Empire, he also tells us of his (sometimes impersonal) memories of life in Austria during and after The Great War, the years of terri</span>
  <span id="freeText9940929562278515565" style="display:none">I have delayed writing this review in hopes that some inspiration will come (perhaps writing it for me) but no, I do have to perform the task from my own brain. This is perhaps one of the most melancholy memoirs I've read. While Zweig provides an often golden-hued picture of the Europe of his youth, the turn of the century Vienna of the final years of the Hapsburg Empire, he also tells us of his (sometimes impersonal) memories of life in Austria during and after The Great War, the years of terrible deprivation in the countries that lost, the rise of National Socialism in Germany, and the Second World War. Unspoken, in the background, is the knowledge that Zweig took his life in 1942 shortly after completing this memoir. Europe was his home and for him it appeared broken beyond hope of recovery or redemption.<br /><br />There is much that is happy and beautiful in this story, tales of poets, artists, novelists, creative persons of all ilks that Zweig sought out as a young man in his travels around Europe. He began writing when only a teenage himself but refined his skills and wrote until his death. He knew many of the great men of the 20th century, championed them and collaborated with many of them. He was open to so much.<br /><br />Why did Zweig decide to die? There are several hints scattered through the memoir. Late in his writing he says:<br /><br /><br /> <i>I have no compunction about admitting that since the<br /> day when I had to depend upon identity papers or passports<br /> that were indeed alien, I ceased to feel as if I quite<br /> belonged to myself.</i> (loc 6637)<br /><br /><br />And:<br /><br /><br /> <i>I, as a case-hardened creature of an age of freedom <br /> and a citizen of the world-republic of my dreams, count <br /> every impression of a rubber-stamp in my passport a stigma,<br /> every one of those hearings and searches a humiliation.</i><br /> (loc 6628)<br /><br /><br />Zweig believed in Europe and a Europe for all Europeans. His experience during the 1930s as Hitler began to tighten his hold in central Europe destroyed a faith that was faltering since World War One. Once he fled Austria shortly before Hitler's invasion, he was a man without a country.<br /><br /><br /> <i>Indeed, nothing makes us more sensible of the<br /> immense relapse into which the world fell after the First<br /> World War than the restrictions on man's freedom of<br /> movement and the diminution of his civil rights. Before<br /> 1914 the earth had belonged to all. People went where<br /> they wished and stayed as long as they pleased. There<br /> were no permits, no visas, and it always gives me<br /> pleasure to astonish the young by telling them that<br /> before 1914 I traveled from Europe to India and to<br /> America without passport and without ever having seen<br /> one.</i> (loc 6600)<br /><br /><br />Zweig seems to have been unable to face the prospect of either watching Hitler succeed or watching Europe have to rebuild again. And perhaps he feared another repetition of the cycle within his lifetime, if he lived longer. <br /><br />But Zweig has left us this memoir which is more a biography of his time and many stories, histories and novels which we can read and enjoy today.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9940929562278515565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1195549996?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1195549996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16060106001718402579">Oh, Zweig offers such a marvellous, seductive portrait of the period 1890-1914 through the eyes of a celebrated Austrian-Jewish writer, that you cannot but enjoy it thoroughly. The whole 'fine fleur' of European culture passes by, and that is impressive. It makes you eager to step into a time machine and jump back to Paris, London, Berlin and Vienna and take part in this intensive cosmopolitan culture from before the First World War.<br/><br/>But, unfortunetaly, a small warning is in place here: Zweig cle</span>
  <span id="freeText16060106001718402579" style="display:none">Oh, Zweig offers such a marvellous, seductive portrait of the period 1890-1914 through the eyes of a celebrated Austrian-Jewish writer, that you cannot but enjoy it thoroughly. The whole 'fine fleur' of European culture passes by, and that is impressive. It makes you eager to step into a time machine and jump back to Paris, London, Berlin and Vienna and take part in this intensive cosmopolitan culture from before the First World War.<br /><br />But, unfortunetaly, a small warning is in place here: Zweig clearly presented a "constructed" image of this age, because he wrote this in the darkest period of his life, just before his suicide (on the run for the nazis). And so he offers a (understandably) romantization. He sketches the period before the First World War way too idyllic, drenched in melancholy, and only focussed on the very tiny, cultural elite. The gruesome life of peasants, labourers, servants and the intense nationalistic, racist and militaristic fever (in which also intellectuals participated) is almost completely kept out of the picture.<br /><br />Of course Zweig uses this idyllic portrait to contrast it to the chaos and barbarity he experienced after the Great War. But history never is black and white. Nevertheless, I must agree, this is a very compelling read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16060106001718402579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_909384572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/909384572" />
    <a title="Elena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5871422-elena"><img alt="Elena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489767140p2/5871422.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/909384572?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elena" href="/user/show/5871422-elena">Elena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer909384572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5668188548471876117">On Stefan Zweig, "Die Welt von gestern": I just put the book down a few moments ago. Normally I like to marinate in a text a while before commenting. Zweig, unlike Thomas Mann, wrote in a spontaneous, fluid, conversational, druckreif style, and deserves an immediate unlabored impression. And the impression is one of overwhelming loss. In fact he lost his world twice. Reading this in 2014, exactly 100 years since his world fell apart the first time, makes me realize how fragile our culture is. Zw</span>
  <span id="freeText5668188548471876117" style="display:none">On Stefan Zweig, "Die Welt von gestern": I just put the book down a few moments ago. Normally I like to marinate in a text a while before commenting. Zweig, unlike Thomas Mann, wrote in a spontaneous, fluid, conversational, druckreif style, and deserves an immediate unlabored impression. And the impression is one of overwhelming loss. In fact he lost his world twice. Reading this in 2014, exactly 100 years since his world fell apart the first time, makes me realize how fragile our culture is. Zweig brings to life the optimism of 1900, new technology, new art, new wealth...and Vienna was the center for painting, music, and literature. When my father was born, Vienna was still the capital of a multinational powerful empire with an emperor, Franz Josef, who wore elaborate regalia and took part in medieval-style processions and ceremonies. The world Zweig grew up in had this seemingly solid imperial foundation, and was generous enough in spirit to support a vibrant explosion of new creative styles in the arts, much of which still seems modern to us today. Zweig was already writing seriously in high school, and published his work to great acclaim in his early twenties. He was friends with Theodor Herzl who published his work in Die neue freie Presse. He also befriended Rilke, another very young and successful author. He obviously had a gift for friendship and created a buoyant kind of intellectual life for himself. Between his family's wealth and his own literary earnings, he had the financial means to buffer his life against the tragedy of World War I which destroyed the Habsburg empire. Instead of military service he worked in the Archives, sort of alternative service. In 1917 bought a lovely old palace on a hill in Salzburg, and spent the worst of the war years over the border. He came home to Salzburg and experienced the horrible privations in the aftermath of the Versailles Treaty. In his little Salzburg mini-castle (Paschinger Schloessl) he wrote some of his best works, and regained his lost world. He became one of the most celebrated writers in the world. On his fiftieth birthday his editor Kippenberg (also Rilke's) gave him a thick catalog listing all of his publications in various editions and translations. While he regained his lost world, not everyone in the German-speaking world recovered from the humiliations at the end of the Great War. There were signs of trouble, but Zweig could not believe that Europeans would repeat the tragedy of 1914. He used his wealth to acquire a famous collection of manuscripts by great literary and musical geniuses, Mozart, Beethoven etc. He specialized in early drafts that showed the original burst of creative thought before it gets reworked and polished. I see this as a reflection of his own spontaneous nature. Meanwhile he records the ominous signs of Nazi influence in small but telling details. Unemployed youths are suddenly wearing expensive uniforms and dashing around town in shiny new automobiles: where does this money come from, and where are these hired guns going? He collaborates with Richard Strauss on an opera, Die schweigsame Frau, and it gets banned because he is from a Jewish background. He leaves for England and enjoys the conversation of his exiled friend Sigmund Freud. His works are all banned in the German language territories, and are only available abroad in pale translations. He went from being one of the most widely read authors of both fiction and nonfiction (numerous biographies) to a nobody, stateless, vulnerable. His recovered-world was gone again, this time forever. In exile in Brazil, he committed suicide soon after completing this heartbreaking memoir, essentially if indirectly murdered by Hitler. I cannot imagine what he went through. The book is written in a calm, even, factual style. Even today, Zweig has not yet recovered his reputation and readership in the United States, although the Germans are rediscovering him. There is no way to put this all right, of course. The world of yesterday is gone, but it helps to recover his stories, his talent for friendship, generosity, his sense of civility, and his love of music and literature. And learn a few lessons in the process. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5668188548471876117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1189143251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16001037420920151827">I read the first hundred pages or so which painted a vivid picture of life in the waning days of the Hapsburg empire, the patronage of arts, the stability and security felt by everyone, the Jewish community's dynamism, and schooling and university. The book continues from there but it leaves Austria and I felt a bit ambivalent to continue since the next events were the fulfillment of many Nazi party policies of aggression. The fact that Zweig and his wife both committed suicide during the war, e</span>
  <span id="freeText16001037420920151827" style="display:none">I read the first hundred pages or so which painted a vivid picture of life in the waning days of the Hapsburg empire, the patronage of arts, the stability and security felt by everyone, the Jewish community's dynamism, and schooling and university. The book continues from there but it leaves Austria and I felt a bit ambivalent to continue since the next events were the fulfillment of many Nazi party policies of aggression. The fact that Zweig and his wife both committed suicide during the war, even though they were in relative safety in a foreign country, makes me fear the remainder of the book will be more about Zweig's despair, and less about evoking the spirit of what it meant to live through Naziism.<br /><br />I am most grateful for learning about the Austrian poet Hugo Von Hofmannsthal. I did not realize that he was viewed by Rilke, Rimbaud, TS Eliot, and others as one of the best poets of that generation. Having read his work now, I can see that the praise is well deserved.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16001037420920151827" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rj00UHDgzHdSsvdRgdz9hD9wQV1u2/F1n8Md9Yh3nqlZ7SR80177bHzRvyRxYlC4/oqO34OBrgcZjvD8FieQYg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Het meest voor de hand liggend was zijn volgende meesterwerk Massa en Macht, maar ik voelde me nog niet klaar voor deze imponerende studie waar 20 jaar aan gewerkt is en waarmee de Nobelprijs voor de Literatuur grotendeels is verdiend. Ik kwam daardoor uit bij zijn hoog aangeschreven biografie, daar Elias Canetti geen andere romans meer heeft geschreven na zijn eerste. Dit is het eerste deel van die autobiografie: De Behouden Tong. \n\nDe enige biografie die ik ooit eerder las is van Carl Lewis, de atletiek legende, die mijn grote held was op de basisschool. Ik was toen 11 jaar, en dat betekent dat ik er nu dus niet helemaal zeker meer van was of dit genre wel iets voor mij zou zijn. Wat bleek: deze autobiografie is bijna magisch. En of dat nou ligt aan de schrijfstijl van Elias Canetti en de wijze waarop hij alleen de meest belangrijke momenten uit zijn leven tot leven brengt, of iets aan autobiografieën in het algemeen, ik kan het nu niet zeggen, maar mijn eigen persoonlijkheid bepalende herinneringen keerde achter elkaar terug in mijn gedachtes. Wat mooi vond ik dat. Het was net zoals bij het Martyrium, waar ik ook na het lezen nog vaak met de materie in mijn hoofd aan het wegdromen was in het verleden.\n\nDit eerste deel van de autobiografie vertelt over de eerste 16 levensjaren van de schrijver. Terwijl voor mij een verhuizing binnen mijn eigen dorp al een apocalyptische ramp geweest zou zijn geweest in mijn jeugd, doet hij zowel Bulgarije, Engeland, Oostenrijk als Zwitserland aan als opgroeiplekken. Omringt door vele culturen en talen ontwikkelt kleine Elias al snel een liefde voor woorden en zinnen. Hij wordt daarbij aangemoedigd door zijn vader die van jongs af aan veel boeken voor hem meeneemt en hem verder stimuleert door elke avond te vragen wat hij gelezen heeft. Na de plotselinge dood van zijn vader neemt zijn moeder deze rol van hem over en onder haar invloed groeit hij uit tot een belezen jongeman die werkelijk overal geïnteresseerd in is. In deze jaren neemt de relatie met zijn moeder een grote rol in zijn leven in, maar ook de invloed die familieleden, schoolkameraden, docenten, schrijvers en verhalen op hem hebben gehad komen uitgebreid aan bod. De meest fascinerende momenten waren voor mij echter de ervaringen met individuen of groepen die hun macht lieten gelden en daardoor als beginnende inspiratie dienden voor zijn eerder genoemde meesterwerken. Het zorgde ervoor dat ik meteen bij hoofdstuk 1 werd gegrepen.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17464439098593854888\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Hij besloot Mein Kampf minutieus door te spitten, telkens met de vraag in het achterhoofd: waarom hebben miljoenen Europeanen zich hierdoor aangetrokken gevoeld? De sleutel bleek te liggen in de rechtszaak die in 1924 tegen Hitler werd gehouden en die zijn doorbraak als politicus betekende. In zijn beeldende vertelstijl mengt Kieft scènes uit het spectaculaire Hitler-proces met beschouwingen over de aantrekkingskracht van de ideeën in Mein Kampf - tot op de dag van vandaag -, waarbij hij       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">About Stefan Zweig</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1190140738p3/25573.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">Stefan Zweig
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,937
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6561430925539969553">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br/><br/>Zweig studied in Austria, France</span>
  <span id="freeText6561430925539969553" style="display:none">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br /><br />Zweig studied in Austria, France, and Germany before settling in Salzburg in 1913. In 1934, driven into exile by the Nazis, he emigrated to England and then, in 1940, to Brazil by way of New York. Finding only growing loneliness and disillusionment in their new surroundings, he and his second wife committed suicide.<br /><br />Zweig's interest in psychology and the teachings of Sigmund Freud led to his most characteristic work, the subtle portrayal of character. Zweig's essays include studies of Honoré de Balzac, Charles Dickens, and Fyodor Dostoyevsky (<i>Drei Meister</i>, 1920; <i>Three Masters</i>) and of Friedrich Hölderlin, Heinrich von Kleist, and Friedrich Nietzsche (<i>Der Kampf mit dem Dämon</i>, 1925; <i>Master Builders</i>). He achieved popularity with <i>Sternstunden der Menschheit</i> (1928; <i>The Tide of Fortune</i>), five historical portraits in miniature. He wrote full-scale, intuitive rather than objective, biographies of the French statesman Joseph Fouché (1929), Mary Stuart (1935), and others. His stories include those in <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i> (1925; <i>Conflicts</i>). He also wrote a psychological novel, <i>Ungeduld des Herzens</i> (1938; <i>Beware of Pity</i>), and translated works of Charles Baudelaire, Paul Verlaine, and Emile Verhaeren.<br /><br />Most recently, his works provided the inspiration for 2014 film <i>The Grand Budapest Hotel</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6561430925539969553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
